tides</a>)</span></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;WebM (360P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 360P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;360p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;640&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;470808&quot; data-framerate=&quot;30000.3&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Tide St. Simons, GA 2018.webm" style="width:220px;height:124px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Tide_St._Simons%2C_GA_2018.webm/220px--Tide_St._Simons%2C_GA_2018.webm.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/Tide_St._Simons%2C_GA_2018.webm" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide_St._Simons,_GA_2018.webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tide coming in, video stops about 1-1/2 hours before high tide</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Tides</b> are the rise and fall of <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea levels</a> caused by the combined effects of the <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitational</a> forces exerted by the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth&#39;s rotation">rotation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tide_table" title="Tide table">Tide tables</a> can be used to find the predicted times and amplitude (or "<a href="/wiki/Tidal_range" title="Tidal range">tidal range</a>") of tides at any given locale. The predictions are influenced by many factors including the alignment of the Sun and Moon, the <a href="/wiki/Tide#Phase_and_amplitude" title="Tide">phase and amplitude of the tide</a> (pattern of tides in the deep ocean), the <a href="/wiki/Amphidromic" class="mw-redirect" title="Amphidromic">amphidromic</a> systems of the oceans, and the shape of the coastline and near-shore <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a> (see <i><a href="/wiki/Tide#Timing" title="Tide">Timing</a></i>).  They are however only predictions, the actual time and height of the tide is affected by wind and atmospheric pressure.  Some shorelines experience a <a href="/wiki/Semi-diurnal" class="mw-redirect" title="Semi-diurnal">semi-diurnal</a> tide\xe2\x80\x94two nearly equal high and low tides each day. Other locations experience a <a href="/wiki/Diurnal_cycle" title="Diurnal cycle">diurnal</a> tide\xe2\x80\x94only one high and low tide each day. A "mixed tide"\xe2\x80\x94two uneven tides a day, or one high and one low\xe2\x80\x94is also possible.<sup id="cite_ref-Reddy_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reddy-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hubbard_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hubbard-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tides vary on timescales ranging from hours to years due to a number of factors, which determine the <a href="/wiki/Lunitidal_interval" title="Lunitidal interval">lunitidal interval</a>. To make accurate records, <a href="/wiki/Tide_gauge" title="Tide gauge">tide gauges</a> at fixed stations measure water level over time. Gauges ignore variations caused by waves with periods shorter than minutes. These data are compared to the reference (or datum) level usually called <a href="/wiki/Mean_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Mean sea level">mean sea level</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While tides are usually the largest source of short-term sea-level fluctuations, sea levels are also subject to forces such as wind and barometric pressure changes, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surges</a>, especially in shallow seas and near coasts.\n</p><p>Tidal phenomena are not limited to the oceans, but can occur in other systems whenever a gravitational field that varies in time and space is present. For example, the shape of the solid part of the Earth is affected slightly by <a href="/wiki/Earth_tide" title="Earth tide">Earth tide</a>, though this is not as easily seen as the water tidal movements.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Definitions</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#Tidal_constituents"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Tidal constituents</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Principal_lunar_semi-diurnal_constituent"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Principal lunar semi-diurnal constituent</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Range_variation:_springs_and_neaps"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Phase_and_amplitude"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Phase and amplitude</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Physics"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Physics</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#History_of_tidal_physics"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">History of tidal physics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Forces"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Forces</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Laplace&#39;s_tidal_equations"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Laplace\'s tidal equations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Amplitude_and_cycle_time"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Amplitude and cycle time</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Dissipation"><span class="tocnumber">3.5</span> <span class="toctext">Dissipation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Bathymetry"><span class="tocnumber">3.6</span> <span class="toctext">Bathymetry</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Observation_and_prediction"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Observation and prediction</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Timing"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Timing</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Analysis"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Analysis</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Example_calculation"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Example calculation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Current"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Current</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Power_generation"><span class="tocnumber">4.6</span> <span class="toctext">Power generation</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Navigation"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Navigation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Biological_aspects"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Biological aspects</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Intertidal_ecology"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Intertidal ecology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Biological_rhythms"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Biological rhythms</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Other_tides"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Other tides</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Lake_tides"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Lake tides</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Atmospheric_tides"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Atmospheric tides</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Earth_tides"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Earth tides</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Galactic_tides"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Galactic tides</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#Misnomers"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Misnomers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>\n<p><span id="Flood_tide"></span><span id="High_tide"></span><span id="Ebb_tide"></span><span id="Low_tide"></span>\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Ebb tide" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Ebb_tide_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Ebb tide (disambiguation)">Ebb tide (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide_type.svg" class="image"><img alt="Three graphs. The first shows the twice-daily rising and falling tide pattern with nearly regular high and low elevations. The second shows the much more variable high and low tides that form a &quot;mixed tide&quot;. The third shows the day-long period of a diurnal tide." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Tide_type.svg/220px-Tide_type.svg.png" width="220" height="334" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Tide_type.svg/330px-Tide_type.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Tide_type.svg/440px-Tide_type.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="539" data-file-height="818" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide_type.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Types of tides (See <i>Timing</i> (below) for coastal map)</div></div></div>\n<p>Tide changes proceed via the following stages:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Sea level rises over several hours, covering the <a href="/wiki/Intertidal_zone" title="Intertidal zone">intertidal zone</a>; <b>flood tide</b>.</li>\n<li>The water rises to its highest level, reaching <b>high tide</b>.</li>\n<li>Sea level falls over several hours, revealing the intertidal zone; <b>ebb tide</b>.</li>\n<li>The water stops falling, reaching <b>low tide</b>.</li></ul>\n<p>Oscillating currents produced by tides are known as tidal streams. The moment that the tidal current ceases is called <a href="/wiki/Slack_water" title="Slack water">slack water</a> or slack tide. The tide then reverses direction and is said to be turning. <b>Slack water</b> usually occurs near high water and low water. But there are locations where the moments of slack tide differ significantly from those of high and low water.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tides are commonly <i>semi-diurnal</i> (two high waters and two low waters each day), or <i>diurnal</i> (one tidal cycle per day). The two high waters on a given day are typically not the same height (the daily inequality); these are the <i>higher high water</i> and the <i>lower high water</i> in <a href="/wiki/Tide_table" title="Tide table">tide tables</a>. Similarly, the two low waters each day are the <i>higher low water</i> and the <i>lower low water</i>. The daily inequality is not consistent and is generally small when the Moon is over the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span></h3>\n<p>From the highest level to the lowest:\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>Highest astronomical tide</b> (<b>HAT</b>) \xe2\x80\x93 The highest tide which can be predicted to occur.  Note that meteorological conditions may add extra height to the HAT.</li>\n<li><b>Mean high water springs</b> (<b>MHWS</b>) \xe2\x80\x93 The average of the two high tides on the days of spring tides.</li>\n<li><b>Mean high water neaps</b> (<b>MHWN</b>) \xe2\x80\x93 The average of the two high tides on the days of neap tides.</li>\n<li><b>Mean sea level</b> (<b>MSL</b>) \xe2\x80\x93 This is the average sea level.  The MSL is constant for any location over a long period.</li>\n<li><b>Mean low water neaps</b> (<b>MLWN</b>) \xe2\x80\x93 The average of the two low tides on the days of neap tides.</li>\n<li><b>Mean low water springs</b> (<b>MLWS</b>) \xe2\x80\x93 The average of the two low tides on the days of spring tides.</li>\n<li><b>Lowest astronomical tide</b> (<b>LAT</b>) and <b>Chart Datum</b> (<b>CD</b>) \xe2\x80\x93 The lowest tide which can be predicted to occur.  Modern charts use this as the chart datum.  Note that under certain meteorological conditions the water may fall lower than this meaning that there is less water than shown on charts.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide_terms.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Tide_terms.png/800px-Tide_terms.png" width="800" height="400" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Tide_terms.png/1200px-Tide_terms.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c1/Tide_terms.png 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide_terms.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration by the course of half a month</div></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tidal_constituents">Tidal constituents</span></h2>\n<p><i>Tidal constituents</i> are the net result of multiple influences impacting tidal changes over certain periods of time.  Primary constituents include the Earth\'s rotation, the position of the Moon and Sun relative to the Earth, the Moon\'s altitude (elevation) above the Earth\'s Equator, and <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a>.  Variations with periods of less than half a day are called <i>harmonic constituents</i>. Conversely, cycles of days, months, or years are referred to as <i>long period</i> constituents.\n</p><p>Tidal forces <a href="/wiki/Earth_tide" title="Earth tide">affect the entire earth</a>, but the movement of solid Earth occurs by mere centimeters.  In contrast, the atmosphere is much more fluid and compressible so its surface moves by kilometers, in the sense of the contour level of a particular low pressure in the outer atmosphere.\n</p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Earth_tide#Tidal_constituents" title="Earth tide">Earth tide \xc2\xa7&#160;Tidal constituents</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Principal_lunar_semi-diurnal_constituent">Principal lunar semi-diurnal constituent</span></h3>\n<p>In most locations, the largest constituent is the "principal lunar semi-diurnal", also known as the <i>M2</i> (or <i>M</i><sub>2</sub>) tidal constituent. Its period is about 12 hours and 25.2 minutes, exactly half a <i>tidal lunar day</i>, which is the average time separating one lunar <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">zenith</a> from the next, and thus is the time required for the Earth to rotate once relative to the Moon. Simple <a href="/wiki/Tide_clock" title="Tide clock">tide clocks</a> track this constituent. The lunar day is longer than the Earth day because the Moon orbits in the same direction the Earth spins. This is analogous to the minute hand on a watch crossing the hour hand at 12:00 and then again at about 1:05\xc2\xbd (not at 1:00).\n</p><p>The Moon orbits the Earth in the same direction as the Earth rotates on its axis, so it takes slightly more than a day\xe2\x80\x94about 24 hours and 50 minutes\xe2\x80\x94for the Moon to return to the same location in the sky. During this time, it has passed overhead (<a href="/wiki/Culmination" title="Culmination">culmination</a>) once and underfoot once (at an <a href="/wiki/Hour_angle" title="Hour angle">hour angle</a> of 00:00 and 12:00 respectively), so in many places the period of strongest tidal forcing is the above-mentioned, about 12 hours and 25 minutes. The moment of highest tide is not necessarily when the Moon is nearest to <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">zenith</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nadir" title="Nadir">nadir</a>, but the period of the forcing still determines the time between high tides.\n</p><p>Because the gravitational field created by the Moon weakens with distance from the Moon, it exerts a slightly stronger than average force on the side of the Earth facing the Moon, and a slightly weaker force on the opposite side. The Moon thus tends to "stretch" the Earth slightly along the line connecting the two bodies. The solid Earth deforms a bit, but ocean water, being fluid, is free to move much more in response to the tidal force, particularly horizontally.  As the Earth rotates, the magnitude and direction of the tidal force at any particular point on the Earth\'s surface change constantly; although the ocean never reaches equilibrium\xe2\x80\x94there is never time for the fluid to "catch up" to the state it would eventually reach if the tidal force were constant\xe2\x80\x94the changing tidal force nonetheless causes rhythmic changes in sea surface height.\n</p><p>When there are two high tides each day with different heights (and two low tides also of different heights), the pattern is called a <i>mixed semi-diurnal tide</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-noaa7_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noaa7-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Range_variation:_springs_and_neaps">Range variation: springs and neaps</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide_schematic.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Tide_schematic.svg/220px-Tide_schematic.svg.png" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Tide_schematic.svg/330px-Tide_schematic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Tide_schematic.svg/440px-Tide_schematic.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="570" data-file-height="319" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide_schematic.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The types of tides</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tidalwaves1.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Tidalwaves1.gif/220px-Tidalwaves1.gif" width="220" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/41/Tidalwaves1.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="304" data-file-height="256" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tidalwaves1.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of tides as the Moon goes round the Earth with the Sun on the right</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tidal_range" title="Tidal range">Tidal range</a></div>\n<p><span id="springtide"></span>The semi-diurnal range (the difference in height between high and low waters over about half a day) varies in a two-week cycle. Approximately twice a month, around <a href="/wiki/New_moon" title="New moon">new moon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Full_moon" title="Full moon">full moon</a> when the Sun, Moon, and Earth form a line (a configuration known as a <a href="/wiki/Syzygy_(astronomy)" title="Syzygy (astronomy)">syzygy</a><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>), the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal force</a> due to the Sun reinforces that due to the Moon. The tide\'s range is then at its maximum; this is called the <b>spring tide</b>. It is not named after the <a href="/wiki/Spring_(season)" title="Spring (season)">season</a>, but, like that word, derives from the meaning "jump, burst forth, rise", as in a natural <a href="/wiki/Spring_(hydrosphere)" class="mw-redirect" title="Spring (hydrosphere)">spring</a>.\n</p><p>When the Moon is at <a href="/wiki/Gibbous" class="mw-redirect" title="Gibbous">first quarter</a> or third quarter, the Sun and Moon are separated by 90\xc2\xb0 when viewed from the Earth, and the solar tidal force partially cancels the Moon\'s tidal force. At these points in the lunar cycle, the tide\'s range is at its minimum; this is called the <b>neap tide</b>, or <b>neaps</b>.  Neap is an Anglo-Saxon word meaning "without the power", as in <i>for\xc4\x91ganges nip</i> (forth-going without-the-power).<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Spring tides result in high waters that are higher than average, low waters that are lower than average, \'<a href="/wiki/Slack_water" title="Slack water">slack water</a>\' time that is shorter than average, and stronger tidal currents than average. Neaps result in less-extreme tidal conditions. There is about a seven-day interval between springs and neaps.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width:
