style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_altitude">Lunar altitude</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bangchuidao_Island.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Bangchuidao_Island.JPG/220px-Bangchuidao_Island.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Bangchuidao_Island.JPG/330px-Bangchuidao_Island.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Bangchuidao_Island.JPG/440px-Bangchuidao_Island.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4896" data-file-height="3672" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bangchuidao_Island.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Low tide at Bangchuidao scenic area, <a href="/wiki/Dalian" title="Dalian">Dalian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liaoning_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Liaoning Province">Liaoning Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Negative_low_tide_at_Ocean_Beach_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Negative_low_tide_at_Ocean_Beach_1.jpg/220px-Negative_low_tide_at_Ocean_Beach_1.jpg" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Negative_low_tide_at_Ocean_Beach_1.jpg/330px-Negative_low_tide_at_Ocean_Beach_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Negative_low_tide_at_Ocean_Beach_1.jpg/440px-Negative_low_tide_at_Ocean_Beach_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1777" data-file-height="1826" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Negative_low_tide_at_Ocean_Beach_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Low tide at <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Beach,_San_Francisco,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="Ocean Beach, San Francisco, California">Ocean Beach</a> in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, U.S.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_coast_at_low_tide,_Bar_Harbor_IMG_2262.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Atlantic_coast_at_low_tide%2C_Bar_Harbor_IMG_2262.JPG/220px-Atlantic_coast_at_low_tide%2C_Bar_Harbor_IMG_2262.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Atlantic_coast_at_low_tide%2C_Bar_Harbor_IMG_2262.JPG/330px-Atlantic_coast_at_low_tide%2C_Bar_Harbor_IMG_2262.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Atlantic_coast_at_low_tide%2C_Bar_Harbor_IMG_2262.JPG/440px-Atlantic_coast_at_low_tide%2C_Bar_Harbor_IMG_2262.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4272" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_coast_at_low_tide,_Bar_Harbor_IMG_2262.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Low tide at <a href="/wiki/Bar_Harbor,_Maine" title="Bar Harbor, Maine">Bar Harbor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>, U.S. (2014)</div></div></div>\n<p>The changing distance separating the Moon and Earth also affects tide heights. When the Moon is closest, at <a href="/wiki/Perigee" class="mw-redirect" title="Perigee">perigee</a>, the range increases, and when it is at <a href="/wiki/Apogee" class="mw-redirect" title="Apogee">apogee</a>, the range shrinks. Every &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap">7<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> <a href="/wiki/Lunation" class="mw-redirect" title="Lunation">lunations</a> (the full cycles from full moon to new to full), perigee coincides with either a new or full moon causing <a href="/wiki/Perigean_spring_tide" title="Perigean spring tide">perigean spring tides</a> with the largest <i><a href="/wiki/Tidal_range" title="Tidal range">tidal range</a></i>. Even at its most powerful this force is still weak,<sup id="cite_ref-Plait2011_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plait2011-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> causing tidal differences of inches at most.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_constituents">Other constituents</span></h3>\n<p>These include solar gravitational effects, the obliquity (tilt) of the Earth\'s Equator and rotational axis, the inclination of the plane of the lunar orbit and the elliptical shape of the Earth\'s orbit of the Sun.\n</p><p>A compound tide (or overtide) results from the shallow-water interaction of its two parent waves.<sup id="cite_ref-leprovost_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leprovost-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phase_and_amplitude">Phase and amplitude</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M2_tidal_constituent.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Map showing relative tidal magnitudes of different ocean areas" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/M2_tidal_constituent.jpg/220px-M2_tidal_constituent.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/M2_tidal_constituent.jpg/330px-M2_tidal_constituent.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/M2_tidal_constituent.jpg/440px-M2_tidal_constituent.jpg 2x" data-file-width="730" data-file-height="470" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M2_tidal_constituent.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>M</i><sub>2</sub> tidal constituent. Amplitude is indicated by color, and the white lines are cotidal differing by 1 hour. The colors indicate where tides are most extreme (highest highs, lowest lows), with blues being least extreme. In almost a dozen places on this map the lines converge. Notice how at each of these places the surrounding color is blue, indicating little or no tide.  These convergent areas are called <a href="/wiki/Amphidromic_point" title="Amphidromic point">amphidromic points</a>. The curved arcs around the amphidromic points show the direction of the tides, each indicating a synchronized 6-hour period. Tidal ranges generally increase with increasing distance from amphidromic points. Tide waves move around these points, generally counterclockwise in the N. Hemisphere and clockwise in the S. Hemisphere <sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Because the <i>M</i><sub>2</sub> tidal constituent dominates in most locations, the stage or <i>phase</i> of a tide, denoted by the time in hours after high water, is a useful concept. Tidal stage is also measured in degrees, with 360\xc2\xb0 per tidal cycle. Lines of constant tidal phase are called <i>cotidal lines</i>, which are analogous to <a href="/wiki/Contour_lines" class="mw-redirect" title="Contour lines">contour lines</a> of constant altitude on <a href="/wiki/Topographical_maps" class="mw-redirect" title="Topographical maps">topographical maps</a>. High water is reached simultaneously along the cotidal lines extending from the coast out into the ocean, and cotidal lines (and hence tidal phases) advance along the coast. Semi-diurnal and long phase constituents are measured from high water, diurnal from maximum flood tide. This and the discussion that follows is precisely true only for a single tidal constituent.\n</p><p>For an ocean in the shape of a circular basin enclosed by a coastline, the <i>cotidal lines</i> point radially inward and must eventually meet at a common point, the <a href="/wiki/Amphidromic_point" title="Amphidromic point">amphidromic point</a>. The amphidromic point is at once cotidal with high and low waters, which is satisfied by <i>zero</i> tidal motion. (The rare exception occurs when the tide encircles an island, as it does around New Zealand, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>.) Tidal motion generally lessens moving away from continental coasts, so that crossing the cotidal lines are contours of constant <i>amplitude</i> (half the distance between high and low water) which decrease to zero at the amphidromic point. For a semi-diurnal tide the amphidromic point can be thought of roughly like the center of a clock face, with the hour hand pointing in the direction of the high water cotidal line, which is directly opposite the low water cotidal line. High water rotates about the amphidromic point once every 12 hours in the direction of rising cotidal lines, and away from ebbing cotidal lines. This rotation, caused by the <a href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a>, is generally clockwise in the southern hemisphere and counterclockwise in the northern hemisphere. The difference of cotidal phase from the phase of a reference tide is the <i>epoch</i>. The reference tide is the hypothetical constituent "equilibrium tide" on a landless Earth measured at 0\xc2\xb0 longitude, the Greenwich meridian.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the North Atlantic, because the cotidal lines circulate counterclockwise around the amphidromic point, the high tide passes New York Harbor approximately an hour ahead of Norfolk Harbor. South of Cape Hatteras the tidal forces are more complex, and cannot be predicted reliably based on the North Atlantic cotidal lines.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">Tidal force</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_tides" title="Theory of tides">Theory of tides</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_tidal_physics">History of tidal physics</span></h3>\n<p>Investigation into tidal physics was important in the early development of <a href="/wiki/Celestial_mechanics" title="Celestial mechanics">celestial mechanics</a>, with the existence of two daily tides being explained by the Moon\'s gravity. Later the daily tides were explained more precisely by the interaction of the Moon\'s and the Sun\'s gravity.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Seleucus_of_Seleucia" title="Seleucus of Seleucia">Seleucus of Seleucia</a> theorized around 150 B.C. that tides were caused by the Moon. The influence of the Moon on bodies of water was also mentioned in <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Tetrabiblos" title="Tetrabiblos">Tetrabiblos</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>.\n</p><p>In <i>De temporum ratione</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Reckoning_of_Time" title="The Reckoning of Time">The Reckoning of Time</a></i>) of 725 <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> linked semidurnal tides and the phenomenon of varying tidal heights to the Moon and its phases.  Bede starts by noting that the tides rise and fall 4/5 of an hour later each day, just as the Moon rises and sets 4/5 of an hour later.<sup id="cite_ref-Wallis_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallis-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>  He goes on to emphasise that in two lunar months (59 days) the Moon circles the Earth 57 times and there are 114 tides.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBede199983_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBede199983-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>  Bede then observes that the height of a tides varies over the month.  Increasing tides are called <i>malinae</i> and decreasing tides <i>ledones</i> and that the month is divided into  four parts of seven or eight days with alternating <i>malinae</i> and <i>ledones</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBede199984_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBede199984-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>  In the same passage he also notes the effect of winds to hold back tides.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBede199984_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBede199984-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Medieval understanding of the tides was primarily based on works of <a href="/wiki/Muslim_astronomers" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim astronomers">Muslim astronomers</a>, which became available through <a href="/wiki/Latin_translations_of_the_12th_century" title="Latin translations of the 12th century">Latin translation</a> starting from the 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Tolmacheva_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tolmacheva-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abu_Ma%27shar" class="mw-redirect" title="Abu Ma&#39;shar">Abu Ma\'shar</a> (d. circa 886), in his <i>Introductorium in astronomiam</i>, taught that ebb and flood tides were caused by the Moon.<sup id="cite_ref-Tolmacheva_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tolmacheva-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Abu Ma\'shar discussed the effects of wind and Moon\'s phases relative to the Sun on the tides.<sup id="cite_ref-Tolmacheva_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tolmacheva-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In the 12th century, <a href="/wiki/Al-Bitruji" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Bitruji">al-Bitruji</a> (d. circa 1204) contributed the notion that the tides were caused by the general circulation of the heavens.<sup id="cite_ref-Tolmacheva_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tolmacheva-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a> in his 1608 <i>De spiegheling der Ebbenvloet</i>, The theory of ebb and flood, dismissed  a large number of misconceptions that still existed about ebb and flood. Stevin pleaded for the idea that the attraction of the Moon was responsible for the tides and spoke in clear terms about ebb, flood, <a href="/wiki/Spring_tide" class="mw-redirect" title="Spring tide">spring tide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neap_tide" class="mw-redirect" title="Neap tide">neap tide</a>, stressing that further research needed to be made.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1609 <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> also correctly suggested that the gravitation of the Moon caused the tides,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> which he based upon ancient observations and correlations.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> in his 1632 <i><a href="/wiki/Dialogue_Concerning_the_Two_Chief_World_Systems" title="Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems">Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</a></i>, whose working title was <i>Dialogue on the Tides</i>, gave an explanation of the tides. The resulting theory, however, was incorrect as he attributed the tides to the sloshing of water caused by the Earth\'s movement around the Sun. He hoped to provide mechanical proof of the Earth\'s movement. The value of his tidal theory is disputed. Galileo rejected Kepler\'s explanation of the tides.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> (1642\xe2\x80\x931727) was the first person to explain tides as the product of the gravitational attraction of astronomical masses. His explanation of the tides (and many other phenomena) was published in the <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophiae_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosophiae Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i> (1687)<sup id="cite_ref-slc-ch2_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slc-ch2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and used his <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton&#39;s law of universal gravitation">theory of universal gravitation</a> to explain the lunar and solar attractions as the origin of the tide-generating forces.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\nNewton and others before <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace" title="Pierre-Simon Laplace">Pierre-Simon Laplace</a> worked the problem from the perspective of a static system (equilibrium theory), that provided an approximation that described the tides that would occur in a non-inertial ocean evenly covering the whole Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-slc-ch2_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slc-ch2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The tide-generating force (or its corresponding <a href="/wiki/Scalar_potential" title="Scalar potential">potential</a>) is still relevant to tidal theory, but as an intermediate quantity (forcing function) rather than as a final result; theory must also consider the Earth\'s accumulated dynamic tidal response to the applied forces, which response is influenced by ocean depth, the Earth\'s rotation, and other factors.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1740, the <a href="/wiki/Acad%C3%A9mie_Royale_des_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Acad\xc3\xa9mie Royale des Sciences">Acad\xc3\xa9mie Royale des Sciences</a> in Paris offered a prize for the best theoretical essay on tides. <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Bernoulli" title="Daniel Bernoulli">Daniel Bernoulli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colin_Maclaurin" title="Colin Maclaurin">Colin Maclaurin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Cavalleri" title="Antoine Cavalleri">Antoine Cavalleri</a> shared the prize.<sup id="cite_ref-EulerAiton1996_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EulerAiton1996-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Maclaurin used Newton\'s theory to show that a smooth sphere covered by a sufficiently deep ocean under the tidal force of a single deforming body is a <a href="/wiki/Prolate" class="mw-redirect" title="Prolate">prolate</a> spheroid (essentially a three-dimensional oval) with major axis directed toward the deforming body. Maclaurin was the first to write about the Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Coriolis effect">rotational effects</a> on motion. Euler realized that the tidal force\'s <i>horizontal</i> component (more than the vertical) drives the tide. In 1744 <a href="/wiki/Jean_le_Rond_d%27Alembert" title="Jean le Rond d&#39;Alembert">Jean le Rond d\'Alembert</a> studied tidal equations for the atmosphere which did not include rotation.\n</p><p>In 1770 <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Barque" title="Barque">barque</a> <a href="/wiki/HMS_Endeavour" title="HMS Endeavour">HMS <i>Endeavour</i></a> grounded on the <a href="/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef" title="Great Barrier Reef">Great Barrier Reef</a>.  Attempts were made to refloat her on the following tide which failed, but the tide after that lifted her clear with ease.  Whilst she was being repaired in the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Endeavour_River" title="Endeavour River">Endeavour River</a> Cook observed the tides over a period of seven weeks.  At neap tides both tides in a day were similar, but at springs the tides rose 7 feet (2.1&#160;m) in the morning but 9 feet (2.7&#160;m) in the evening.<sup id="cite_ref-Cook_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cook-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pierre-Simon Laplace formulated a system of <a href="/wiki/Partial_differential_equation" title="Partial differential equation">partial differential equations</a> relating the ocean\'s horizontal flow to its surface height, the first major dynamic theory for water tides. The <a href="/wiki/Laplace%27s_tidal_equations" class="mw-redirect" title="Laplace&#39;s tidal equations">Laplace tidal equations</a> are still in use today. <a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin</a>, rewrote Laplace\'s equations in terms of <a href="/wiki/Vorticity" title="Vorticity">vorticity</a> which allowed for solutions describing tidally driven coastally trapped waves, known as <a href="/wiki/Kelvin_wave" title="Kelvin wave">Kelvin waves</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tidhist_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tidhist-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Others including Kelvin and <a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincar\xc3\xa9">Henri Poincar\xc3\xa9</a> further developed Laplace\'s theory. Based on these developments and the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_theory" title="Lunar theory">lunar theory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ernest_William_Brown" title="Ernest William Brown">E W Brown</a> describing the motions of the Moon, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Thomas_Doodson" title="Arthur Thomas Doodson">Arthur Thomas Doodson</a> developed and published in 1921<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> the first modern development of the tide-generating potential in harmonic form: Doodson distinguished 388 tidal frequencies.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Some of his methods remain in use.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forces">Forces</span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal force</a> produced by a massive object (Moon, hereafter) on a small particle located on or in an extensive body (Earth, hereafter) is the vector difference between the gravitational force exerted by the Moon on the particle, and the gravitational force that would be exerted on the particle if it were located at the Earth\'s center of mass. \n</p><p>Whereas the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitational force">gravitational force</a> subjected by a celestial body on Earth varies inversely as the square of its distance to the Earth, the maximal tidal force varies inversely as, approximately, the cube of this distance.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> If the tidal force caused by each body were instead equal to its full gravitational force (which is not the case due to the <a href="/wiki/Free_fall" title="Free fall">free fall</a> of the whole Earth, not only the oceans, towards these bodies) a different pattern of tidal forces would be observed, e.g. with a much stronger influence from the Sun than from the Moon: The solar gravitational force on the Earth is on average 179 times stronger than the lunar, but because the Sun is on average 389 times farther from the Earth, its field gradient is weaker. The solar tidal force is 46% as large as the lunar.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> More precisely, the lunar tidal acceleration (along the Moon\xe2\x80\x93Earth axis, at the Earth\'s surface) is about 1.1 \xc3\x97 10<sup>\xe2\x88\x927</sup> <i>g</i>, while the solar tidal acceleration (along the Sun\xe2\x80\x93Earth axis, at the Earth\'s surface) is about 0.52 \xc3\x97 10<sup>\xe2\x88\x927</sup> <i>g</i>, where <i>g</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Standard_gravity" title="Standard gravity">gravitational acceleration</a> at the Earth\'s surface.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Venus has the largest effect of the other planets, at 0.000113 times the solar effect. The system of the Earth, the Moon and the Sun is an example of a <a href="/wiki/Three-body_problem" title="Three-body problem">three-body problem</a>, and there is no exact mathematical <a href="/wiki/Closed-form_expression" title="Closed-form expression">closed-form expression</a> of their interdependence.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Field_tidal.svg" class="image"><img alt="Diagram showing a circle with closely spaced arrows pointing away from the reader on the left and right sides, while pointing towards the user on the top and bottom." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Field_tidal.svg/220px-Field_tidal.svg.png" width="220" height="119" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Field_tidal.svg/330px-Field_tidal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Field_tidal.svg/440px-Field_tidal.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1123" data-file-height="608" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Field_tidal.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The lunar <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> differential <a href="/wiki/Vector_field" title="Vector field">field</a> at the Earth\'s surface is known as the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tide-generating force</a>. This is the primary mechanism that drives tidal action and explains two equipotential tidal bulges, accounting for two daily high waters.</div></div></div>\n<p>The ocean\'s surface is closely approximated by an equipotential surface, (ignoring ocean currents) commonly referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Geoid" title="Geoid">geoid</a>. Since the gravitational force is equal to the potential\'s <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">gradient</a>, there are no <a href="/wiki/Tangent" title="Tangent">tangential</a> forces on such a surface, and the ocean surface is thus in gravitational equilibrium. Now consider the effect of massive external bodies such as the Moon and Sun. These bodies have strong gravitational fields that diminish with distance and act to alter the shape of an equipotential surface on the Earth. This deformation has a fixed spatial orientation relative to the influencing body. The Earth\'s rotation relative to this shape causes the daily tidal cycle. The ocean surface moves because of the changing tidal equipotential, rising when the tidal potential is high, which occurs on the parts of the Earth nearest to and furthest from the Moon. When the tidal equipotential changes, the ocean surface is no longer aligned with it, so the apparent direction of the vertical shifts. The surface then experiences a down slope, in the direction that the equipotential has risen.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Laplace.27s_tidal_equations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Laplace\'s_tidal_equations">Laplace\'s tidal equations</span></h3>\n<p>Ocean depths are much smaller than their horizontal extent. Thus, the response to tidal forcing can be <a href="/wiki/Model_(abstract)" class="mw-redirect" title="Model (abstract)">modelled</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Laplace%27s_tidal_equations" class="mw-redirect" title="Laplace&#39;s tidal equations">Laplace tidal equations</a> which incorporate the following features:\n</p>\n<ol><li>The vertical (or radial) velocity is negligible, and there is no vertical <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">shear</a>\xe2\x80\x94this is a sheet flow.</li>\n<li>The forcing is only horizontal (<a href="/wiki/Tangent" title="Tangent">tangential</a>).</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a> appears as an inertial force (fictitious) acting laterally to the direction of flow and proportional to velocity.</li>\n<li>The surface height\'s rate of change is proportional to the negative divergence of velocity multiplied by the depth. As the horizontal velocity stretches or compresses the ocean as a sheet, the volume thins or thickens, respectively.</li></ol>\n<p>The boundary conditions dictate no flow across the coastline and free slip at the bottom.\n</p><p>The Coriolis effect (inertial force) steers flows moving towards the Equator to the west and flows moving away from the Equator toward the east, allowing coastally trapped waves. Finally, a dissipation term can be added which is an analog to viscosity.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amplitude_and_cycle_time">Amplitude and cycle time</span></h3>\n<p>The theoretical amplitude of oceanic tides caused by the Moon is about 54 centimetres (21&#160;in) at the highest point, which corresponds to the amplitude that would be reached if the ocean possessed a uniform depth, there were no landmasses, and the Earth were rotating in step with the Moon\'s orbit. The Sun similarly causes tides, of which the theoretical amplitude is about 25 centimetres (9.8&#160;in) (46% of that of the Moon) with a cycle time of 12 hours. At spring tide the two effects add to each other to a theoretical level of 79 centimetres (31&#160;in), while at neap tide the theoretical level is reduced to 29 centimetres (11&#160;in). Since the orbits of the Earth about the Sun, and the Moon about the Earth, are elliptical, tidal amplitudes change somewhat as a result of the varying Earth\xe2\x80\x93Sun and Earth\xe2\x80\x93Moon distances. This causes a variation in the tidal force and theoretical amplitude of about \xc2\xb118% for the Moon and \xc2\xb15% for the Sun. If both the Sun and Moon were at their closest positions and aligned at new moon, the theoretical amplitude would reach 93 centimetres (37&#160;in).\n</p><p>Real amplitudes differ considerably, not only because of depth variations and continental obstacles, but also because wave propagation across the ocean has a natural period of the same order of magnitude as the rotation period: if there were no land masses, it would take about 30 hours for a long wavelength surface wave to propagate along the Equator halfway around the Earth (by comparison, the Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a> has a natural period of about 57 minutes). <a href="/wiki/Earth_tide" title="Earth tide">Earth tides</a>, which raise and lower the bottom of the ocean, and the tide\'s own gravitational self attraction are both significant and further complicate the ocean\'s response to tidal forces.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dissipation">Dissipation</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">Tidal acceleration</a></div>\n<p>Earth\'s tidal oscillations introduce dissipation at an <a href="/wiki/Average" title="Average">average</a> rate of about 3.75 <a href="/wiki/Terawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Terawatt">terawatts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Munk1998_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Munk1998-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\nAbout 98% of this dissipation is by marine tidal movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Ray1996_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ray1996-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\nDissipation arises as basin-scale tidal flows drive smaller-scale flows which experience turbulent dissipation. This tidal drag creates torque on the moon that gradually transfers angular momentum to its orbit, and a gradual increase in Earth\xe2\x80\x93moon separation. The equal and opposite torque on the Earth correspondingly decreases its rotational velocity. Thus, over geologic time, the moon recedes from the Earth, at about 3.8 centimetres (1.5&#160;in)/year, lengthening the terrestrial day.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">Day length has increased</a> by about 2 hours in the last 600 million years. Assuming (as a crude approximation) that the deceleration rate has been constant, this would imply that 70 million years ago, day length was on the order of 1% shorter with about 4 more days per year.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bathymetry">Bathymetry</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gorey_Harbour_at_low_tide.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Gorey_Harbour_at_low_tide.JPG/220px-Gorey_Harbour_at_low_tide.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Gorey_Harbour_at_low_tide.JPG/330px-Gorey_Harbour_at_low_tide.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Gorey_Harbour_at_low_tide.JPG/440px-Gorey_Harbour_at_low_tide.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gorey_Harbour_at_low_tide.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The harbour of <a href="/wiki/Gorey,_Jersey" title="Gorey, Jersey">Gorey, Jersey</a> falls dry at low tide.</div></div></div>\n<p>The shape of the shoreline and the ocean floor changes the way that tides propagate, so there is no simple, general rule that predicts the time of high water from the Moon\'s position in the sky. Coastal characteristics such as underwater <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a> and coastline shape mean that individual location characteristics affect tide forecasting; actual high water time and height may differ from model predictions due to the coastal morphology\'s effects on tidal flow. However, for a given location the relationship between lunar <a href="/wiki/Altitude_(astronomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Altitude (astronomy)">altitude</a> and the time of high or low tide (the <a href="/wiki/Lunitidal_interval" title="Lunitidal interval">lunitidal interval</a>) is relatively constant and predictable, as is the time of high or low tide relative to other points on the same coast. For example, the high tide at <a href="/wiki/Norfolk,_Virginia" title="Norfolk, Virginia">Norfolk, Virginia</a>, U.S., predictably occurs approximately two and a half hours before the Moon passes directly overhead.\n</p><p>Land masses and ocean basins act as barriers against water moving freely around the globe, and their varied shapes and sizes affect the size of tidal frequencies. As a result, tidal patterns vary. For example, in the U.S., the East coast has predominantly semi-diurnal tides, as do Europe\'s Atlantic coasts, while the West coast predominantly has mixed tides.<sup id="cite_ref-noaa7b_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noaa7b-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation_and_prediction">Observation and prediction</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Compass_bearing_of_high_waters_in_the_Bay_of_Biscay_left_Brittany_to_Dover_right.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Compass_bearing_of_high_waters_in_the_Bay_of_Biscay_left_Brittany_to_Dover_right.jpg/220px-Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Compass_bearing_of_high_waters_in_the_Bay_of_Biscay_left_Brittany_to_Dover_right.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Compass_bearing_of_high_waters_in_the_Bay_of_Biscay_left_Brittany_to_Dover_right.jpg/330px-Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Compass_bearing_of_high_waters_in_the_Bay_of_Biscay_left_Brittany_to_Dover_right.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Compass_bearing_of_high_waters_in_the_Bay_of_Biscay_left_Brittany_to_Dover_right.jpg/440px-Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Compass_bearing_of_high_waters_in_the_Bay_of_Biscay_left_Brittany_to_Dover_right.jpg 2x" data-file-width="622" data-file-height="429" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Compass_bearing_of_high_waters_in_the_Bay_of_Biscay_left_Brittany_to_Dover_right.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Brouscon" title="Guillaume Brouscon">Brouscon\'s Almanach</a> of 1546: Compass bearings of high waters in the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Biscay" title="Bay of Biscay">Bay of Biscay</a> (left) and the coast from <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a> to <a href="/wiki/Dover" title="Dover">Dover</a> (right).</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Tidal_diagrams_according_to_the_age_of_the_Moon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Tidal_diagrams_according_to_the_age_of_the_Moon.jpg/220px-Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Tidal_diagrams_according_to_the_age_of_the_Moon.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Tidal_diagrams_according_to_the_age_of_the_Moon.jpg/330px-Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Tidal_diagrams_according_to_the_age_of_the_Moon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Tidal_diagrams_according_to_the_age_of_the_Moon.jpg/440px-Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Tidal_diagrams_according_to_the_age_of_the_Moon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="622" data-file-height="442" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brouscon_Almanach_1546_Tidal_diagrams_according_to_the_age_of_the_Moon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Brouscon\'s Almanach of 1546: Tidal diagrams "according to the age of the moon".</div></div></div>\n<p>From ancient times, tidal observation and discussion has increased in  sophistication, first marking the daily recurrence, then tides\' relationship to the Sun and moon. <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">Pytheas</a> travelled to the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> about 325 BC and seems to be the first to have related spring tides to the phase of the moon.\n</p><p>In the 2nd century BC, the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_astronomy" title="Babylonian astronomy">Babylonian astronomer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_of_Seleucia" title="Seleucus of Seleucia">Seleucus of Seleucia</a>, correctly described the phenomenon of tides in order to support his <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric</a> theory.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> He correctly theorized that tides were caused by the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">moon</a>, although he believed that the interaction was mediated by the <a href="/wiki/Pneuma" title="Pneuma">pneuma</a>. He noted that tides varied in time and strength in different parts of the world. According to <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> (1.1.9), Seleucus was the first to link tides to the lunar attraction, and that the height of the tides depends on the moon\'s position relative to the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)"><i>Naturalis Historia</i></a> of <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> collates many tidal observations, e.g., the spring tides are a few days after (or before) new and full moon and are highest around the equinoxes, though Pliny noted many relationships now regarded as fanciful. In his <i>Geography</i>, Strabo described tides in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> having their greatest range when the moon was furthest from the plane of the Equator. All this despite the relatively small amplitude of <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> basin tides. (The strong currents through the <a href="/wiki/Euripus_Strait" title="Euripus Strait">Euripus Strait</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Messina" title="Strait of Messina">Strait of Messina</a> puzzled <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>.) <a href="/wiki/Philostratus" title="Philostratus">Philostratus</a> discussed tides in Book Five of <i>The Life of <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Tyana" title="Apollonius of Tyana">Apollonius of Tyana</a></i>. Philostratus mentions the moon, but attributes tides to "spirits". In Europe around 730 AD, the Venerable <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> described how the rising tide on one coast of the British Isles coincided with the fall on the other and described the time progression of high water along the Northumbrian coast.\n</p><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Tide_table" title="Tide table">tide table</a> in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> was recorded in 1056 AD primarily for visitors wishing to see the famous <a href="/wiki/Tidal_bore" title="Tidal bore">tidal bore</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Qiantang_River" title="Qiantang River">Qiantang River</a>. The first known British tide table is thought to be that of John Wallingford, who died Abbot of St. Albans in 1213, based on high water occurring 48 minutes later each day, and three hours earlier at the <a href="/wiki/Thames" class="mw-redirect" title="Thames">Thames</a> mouth than upriver at <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">William Thomson (Lord Kelvin)</a> led the first systematic <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_analysis" title="Harmonic analysis">harmonic analysis</a> of tidal records starting in 1867. The main result was the building of a <a href="/wiki/Tide-predicting_machine" title="Tide-predicting machine">tide-predicting machine</a> using a system of pulleys to add together six harmonic time functions. It was "programmed" by resetting gears and chains to adjust phasing and amplitudes. Similar machines were used until the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first known sea-level record of an entire spring\xe2\x80\x93neap cycle was made in 1831 on the Navy Dock in the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Estuary" title="Thames Estuary">Thames Estuary</a>. Many large ports had automatic tide gauge stations by 1850.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_Whewell" title="William Whewell">William Whewell</a> first mapped co-tidal lines ending with a nearly global chart in 1836. In order to make these maps consistent, he hypothesized the existence of amphidromes where co-tidal lines meet in the mid-ocean. These points of no tide were confirmed by measurement in 1840 by Captain Hewett, RN, from careful soundings in the North Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-tidhist_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tidhist-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timing">Timing</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diurnal_tide_types_map.jpg" class="image"><img alt="World map showing the location of diurnal, semi-diurnal, and mixed semi-diurnal tides. The European and African west coasts are exclusively semi-diurnal, and North America&#39;s West coast is mixed semi-diurnal, but elsewhere the different patterns are highly intermixed, although a given pattern may cover 200\xe2\x80\x932,000 kilometres (120\xe2\x80\x931,240&#160;mi)." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Diurnal_tide_types_map.jpg/220px-Diurnal_tide_types_map.jpg" width="220" height="108" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Diurnal_tide_types_map.jpg/330px-Diurnal_tide_types_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Diurnal_tide_types_map.jpg/440px-Diurnal_tide_types_map.jpg 2x" data-file-width="855" data-file-height="420" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diurnal_tide_types_map.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The same tidal forcing has different results depending on many factors, including coast orientation, continental shelf margin, water body dimensions.</div></div></div>\n<p>The tidal forces due to the Moon and Sun generate very long  waves which travel all around the ocean following the paths shown in <a href="/wiki/Tide#Phase_and_amplitude" title="Tide">co-tidal charts</a>. The time when the crest of the wave reaches a port then gives the time of high water at the port. The time taken for the wave to travel around the ocean also means that there is a delay between the phases of the Moon and their effect on the tide. Springs and neaps in the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, for example, are two days behind the new/full moon and first/third quarter moon. This is called the tide\'s <i>age</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The ocean <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a> greatly influences the tide\'s exact time and height at a particular <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">coastal</a> point. There are some extreme cases; the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Fundy" title="Bay of Fundy">Bay of Fundy</a>, on the east coast of Canada, is often stated to have the world\'s highest tides because of its shape, bathymetry, and its distance from the continental shelf edge.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Measurements made in November 1998 at Burntcoat Head in the Bay of Fundy recorded a maximum range of 16.3 metres (53&#160;ft) and a highest predicted extreme of 17 metres (56&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-BIO2004_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BIO2004-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FundyWorkshop_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FundyWorkshop-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\nSimilar measurements made in March 2002 at Leaf Basin, <a href="/wiki/Ungava_Bay" title="Ungava Bay">Ungava Bay</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a> gave similar values (allowing for measurement errors), a maximum range of 16.2 metres (53&#160;ft) and a highest predicted extreme of 16.8 metres (55&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-BIO2004_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BIO2004-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FundyWorkshop_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FundyWorkshop-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>  Ungava Bay and the Bay of Fundy lie similar distances from the continental shelf edge, but Ungava Bay is free of <a href="/wiki/Pack_ice" class="mw-redirect" title="Pack ice">pack ice</a> for about four months every year while the Bay of Fundy rarely freezes.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a> in the United Kingdom has a double high water caused by the interaction between the <i>M</i><sub>2</sub> and <i>M</i><sub>4</sub> tidal constituents.<sup id="cite_ref-Pingree_1978_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pingree_1978-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Portland" title="Isle of Portland">Portland</a> has double low waters for the same reason.  The <i>M</i><sub>4</sub> tide is found all along the south coast of the United Kingdom, but its effect is most noticeable between the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Portland" title="Isle of Portland">Portland</a> because the <i>M</i><sub>2</sub> tide is lowest in this region.\n</p><p>Because the oscillation modes of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> do not coincide with any significant astronomical forcing period, the largest tides are close to their narrow connections with the Atlantic Ocean. Extremely small tides also occur for the same reason in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Japan" title="Sea of Japan">Sea of Japan</a>. Elsewhere, as along the southern coast of <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, low tides can be due to the presence of a nearby <a href="/wiki/Amphidromic_point" title="Amphidromic point">amphidrome</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis">Analysis</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Water_surface_level_changes_with_tides.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Water_surface_level_changes_with_tides.svg/220px-Water_surface_level_changes_with_tides.svg.png" width="220" height="64" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Water_surface_level_changes_with_tides.svg/330px-Water_surface_level_changes_with_tides.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Water_surface_level_changes_with_tides.svg/440px-Water_surface_level_changes_with_tides.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2750" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Water_surface_level_changes_with_tides.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A regular water level chart</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>\'s theory of gravitation first enabled an explanation of why there were generally two tides a day, not one, and offered hope for a detailed understanding of tidal forces and behavior. Although it may seem that tides could be predicted via a sufficiently detailed knowledge of instantaneous astronomical forcings, the actual tide at a given location is determined by astronomical forces accumulated over many days. In addition, precise results require detailed knowledge of the shape of all the ocean basins\xe2\x80\x94their <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a>, and coastline shape.\n</p><p>Current procedure for analysing tides follows the method of harmonic analysis introduced in the 1860s by <a href="/wiki/Lord_Kelvin" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Kelvin">William Thomson</a>. It is based on the principle that the astronomical theories of the motions of Sun and Moon determine a large number of component frequencies, and at each frequency there is a component of force tending to produce tidal motion, but that at each place of interest on the Earth, the tides respond at each frequency with an amplitude and phase peculiar to that locality.  At each place of interest, the tide heights are therefore measured for a period of time sufficiently long (usually more than a year in the case of a new port not previously studied) to enable the response at each significant tide-generating frequency to be distinguished by analysis, and to extract the tidal constants for a sufficient number of the strongest known components of the astronomical tidal forces to enable practical tide prediction. The tide heights are expected to follow the tidal force, with a constant amplitude and phase delay for each component.  Because astronomical frequencies and phases can be calculated with certainty, the tide height at other times can then be predicted once the response to the harmonic components of the astronomical tide-generating forces has been found.\n</p><p>The main patterns in the tides are\n</p>\n<ul><li>the twice-daily variation</li>\n<li>the difference between the first and second tide of a day</li>\n<li>the spring\xe2\x80\x93neap cycle</li>\n<li>the annual variation</li></ul>\n<p>The <i>Highest Astronomical Tide</i> is the perigean spring tide when both the Sun and Moon are closest to the Earth.\n</p><p>When confronted by a periodically varying function, the standard approach is to employ <a href="/wiki/Fourier_series" title="Fourier series">Fourier series</a>, a form of analysis that uses <a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">sinusoidal</a> functions as a <i>basis</i> set, having frequencies that are zero, one, two, three, etc. times the frequency of a particular fundamental cycle. These multiples are called <i>harmonics</i> of the fundamental frequency, and the process is termed <a href="/wiki/Harmonic_analysis" title="Harmonic analysis">harmonic analysis</a>. If the basis set of sinusoidal functions suit the behaviour being modelled, relatively few harmonic terms need to be added. Orbital paths are very nearly circular, so sinusoidal variations are suitable for tides.\n</p><p>For the analysis of tide heights, the Fourier series approach has in practice to be made more elaborate than the use of a single frequency and its harmonics.  The tidal patterns are decomposed into many sinusoids having many fundamental frequencies, corresponding (as in the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_theory#Results_of_the_theories" title="Lunar theory">lunar theory</a>) to many different combinations of the motions of the Earth, the Moon, and the angles that define the shape and location of their orbits.\n</p><p>For tides, then, <i>harmonic analysis</i> is not limited to harmonics of a single frequency.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In other words, the harmonies are multiples of many fundamental frequencies, not just of the fundamental frequency of the simpler Fourier series approach.  Their representation as a Fourier series having only one fundamental frequency and its (integer) multiples would require many terms, and would be severely limited in the time-range for which it would be valid.\n</p><p>The study of tide height by harmonic analysis was begun by Laplace, William Thomson (Lord Kelvin), and <a href="/wiki/George_Darwin" title="George Darwin">George Darwin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Thomas_Doodson" title="Arthur Thomas Doodson">A.T. Doodson</a> extended their work, introducing the <i>Doodson Number</i> notation to organise the hundreds of resulting terms. This approach has been the international standard ever since, and the complications arise as follows: the tide-raising force is notionally given by sums of several terms. Each term is of the form\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle A\\cos \\,(\\omega t+p)}">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>A</mi>\n        <mi>cos</mi>\n        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mi>&#x03C9;<!-- \xcf\x89 --></mi>\n        <mi>t</mi>\n        <mo>+</mo>\n        <mi>p</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle A\\cos \\,(\\omega t+p)}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/66122b0e53d8f655d262b8996d19ff547cf9432a" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:13.733ex; height:2.843ex;" alt=" A \\cos\\,(\\omega t + p) "/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <span class="texhtml"><var>A</var></span> is the amplitude, <span class="texhtml"><var>&#969;</var></span> is the angular frequency usually given in degrees per hour corresponding to <span class="texhtml"><var>t</var></span> measured in hours, and <span class="texhtml"><var>p</var></span> is the phase offset with regard to the astronomical state at time <i>t</i> = 0&#160;. There is one term for the Moon and a second term for the Sun. The phase <span class="texhtml"><var>p</var></span> of the first harmonic for the Moon term is called the <a href="/wiki/Lunitidal_interval" title="Lunitidal interval">lunitidal interval</a> or high water interval. The next step is to accommodate the harmonic terms due to the elliptical shape of the orbits. Accordingly, the value of <span class="texhtml"><var>A</var></span> is not a constant but also varying with time, slightly, about some average figure. Replace it then by <span class="texhtml"><var>A</var>(<var>t</var>)</span> where A is another sinusoid, similar to the cycles and epicycles of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemaic theory</a>. Accordingly,\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle A(t)=A{\\bigl (}1+A_{a}\\cos \\,(\\omega _{a}t+p_{a}){\\bigr )}}">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>A</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mi>t</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mi>A</mi>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-OPEN">\n            <mo maxsize="1.2em" minsize="1.2em">(</mo>\n          </mrow>\n        </mrow>\n        <mn>1</mn>\n        <mo>+</mo>\n        <msub>\n          <mi>A</mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mi>a</mi>\n          </mrow>\n        </msub>\n        <mi>cos</mi>\n        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <msub>\n          <mi>&#x03C9;<!-- \xcf\x89 --></mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mi>a</mi>\n          </mrow>\n        </msub>\n        <mi>t</mi>\n        <mo>+</mo>\n        <msub>\n          <mi>p</mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mi>a</mi>\n          </mrow>\n        </msub>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-CLOSE">\n            <mo maxsize="1.2em" minsize="1.2em">)</mo>\n          </mrow>\n        </mrow>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle A(t)=A{\\bigl (}1+A_{a}\\cos \\,(\\omega _{a}t+p_{a}){\\bigr )}}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d6d58f87b40d774d23b9d70df0b7edc4a125101c" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:32.405ex; height:3.176ex;" alt=" A(t) = A\\bigl(1 + A_a \\cos\\,(\\omega_a t + p_a)\\bigr) "/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>which is to say an average value <span class="texhtml"><var>A</var></span> with a sinusoidal variation about it of magnitude <span class="texhtml"><var>A<sub>a</sub></var></span>, with frequency <span class="texhtml"><var>&#969;<sub>a</sub></var></span> and phase <span class="texhtml"><var>p<sub>a</sub></var></span>. Thus the simple term is now the product of two cosine factors:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle A{\\bigl [}1+A_{a}\\cos \\,(\\omega _{a}t+p_{a}){\\bigr ]}\\cos \\,(\\omega t+p)}">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>A</mi>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-OPEN">\n            <mo maxsize="1.2em" minsize="1.2em">[</mo>\n          </mrow>\n        </mrow>\n        <mn>1</mn>\n        <mo>+</mo>\n        <msub>\n          <mi>A</mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mi>a</mi>\n          </mrow>\n        </msub>\n        <mi>cos</mi>\n        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <msub>\n          <mi>&#x03C9;<!-- \xcf\x89 --></mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mi>a</mi>\n          </mrow>\n        </msub>\n        <mi>t</mi>\n        <mo>+</mo>\n        <msub>\n          <mi>p</mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mi>a</mi>\n          </mrow>\n        </msub>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-CLOSE">\n            <mo maxsize="1.2em" minsize="1.2em">]</mo>\n          </mrow>\n        </mrow>\n        <mi>cos</mi>\n        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mi>&#x03C9;<!-- \xcf\x89 --></mi>\n        <mi>t</mi>\n        <mo>+</mo>\n        <mi>p</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle A{\\bigl [}1+A_{a}\\cos \\,(\\omega _{a}t+p_{a}){\\bigr ]}\\cos \\,(\\omega t+p)}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/27dd605f09c7af15645266adc3d30cbe8a22640a" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:36.714ex; height:3.176ex;" alt=" A \\bigl[ 1 + A_a \\cos\\,(\\omega_a t + p_a)\\bigr] \\cos\\,(\\omega t + p) "/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Given that for any <span class="texhtml"><var>x</var></span> and <span class="texhtml"><var>y</var></span>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle \\cos x\\cos y={\\textstyle {\\frac {1}{2}}}\\cos \\,(x+y)+{\\textstyle {\\frac {1}{2}}}\\cos \\,(x-y)}">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>cos</mi>\n        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n        <mi>x</mi>\n        <mi>cos</mi>\n        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n        <mi>y</mi>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n              <mfrac>\n                <mn>1</mn>\n                <mn>2</mn>\n              </mfrac>\n            </mrow>\n          </mstyle>\n        </mrow>\n        <mi>cos</mi>\n        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mi>x</mi>\n        <mo>+</mo>\n        <mi>y</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mo>+</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n              <mfrac>\n                <mn>1</mn>\n                <mn>2</mn>\n              </mfrac>\n            </mrow>\n          </mstyle>\n        </mrow>\n        <mi>cos</mi>\n        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mi>x</mi>\n        <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n        <mi>y</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\cos x\\cos y={\\textstyle {\\frac {1}{2}}}\\cos \\,(x+y)+{\\textstyle {\\frac {1}{2}}}\\cos \\,(x-y)}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c635a458a16dba698371d0040400e8729b218565" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:41.164ex; height:3.509ex;" alt="\\cos x \\cos y = {\\textstyle\\frac{1}{2}} \\cos\\,( x + y ) + {\\textstyle\\frac{1}{2}} \\cos\\,( x - y )"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>it is clear that a compound term involving the product of two cosine terms each with their own frequency is the same as <i>three</i> simple cosine terms that are to be added at the original frequency and also at frequencies which are the sum and difference of the two frequencies of the product term. (Three, not two terms, since the whole expression is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle (1+\\cos x)\\cos y}">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mn>1</mn>\n        <mo>+</mo>\n        <mi>cos</mi>\n        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n        <mi>x</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mi>cos</mi>\n        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n        <mi>y</mi>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle (1+\\cos x)\\cos y}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cecfb324941d764f26c8f8b9a869b8687e17beaf" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:15.681ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="(1 + \\cos x)\\cos y"/></span>.) Consider further that the tidal force on a location depends also on whether the Moon (or the Sun) is above or below the plane of the Equator, and that these attributes have their own periods also incommensurable with a day and a month, and it is clear that many combinations result. With a careful choice of the basic astronomical frequencies, the Doodson Number annotates the particular additions and differences to form the frequency of each simple cosine term.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tidal_constituent_sum.gif" class="image"><img alt="Graph showing one line each for M&#160;2, S&#160;2, N&#160;2, K&#160;1, O&#160;1, P&#160;1, and one for their summation, with the X axis spanning slightly more than a single day" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Tidal_constituent_sum.gif/220px-Tidal_constituent_sum.gif" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/Tidal_constituent_sum.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="253" data-file-height="322" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tidal_constituent_sum.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tidal prediction summing constituent parts.</div></div></div>\n<p>Remember that astronomical tides do <i>not</i> include weather effects. Also, changes to local conditions (sandbank movement, dredging harbour mouths, etc.) away from those prevailing at the measurement time affect the tide\'s actual timing and magnitude. Organisations quoting a "highest astronomical tide" for some location may exaggerate the figure as a safety factor against analytical uncertainties, distance from the nearest measurement point, changes since the last observation time, ground subsidence, etc., to avert liability should an engineering work be overtopped. Special care is needed when assessing the size of a "weather surge" by subtracting the astronomical tide from the observed tide.\n</p><p>Careful Fourier <a href="/wiki/Data_analysis" title="Data analysis">data analysis</a> over a nineteen-year period (the <i>National Tidal Datum Epoch</i> in the U.S.) uses frequencies called the <i>tidal harmonic constituents</i>. Nineteen years is preferred because the Earth, Moon and Sun\'s relative positions repeat almost exactly in the <a href="/wiki/Metonic_cycle" title="Metonic cycle">Metonic cycle</a> of 19&#160;years, which is long enough to include the 18.613 year lunar nodal <a href="/wiki/Earth_tide#Tidal_constituents" title="Earth tide">tidal constituent</a>. This analysis can be done using only the knowledge of the forcing <i>period</i>, but without detailed understanding of the mathematical derivation, which means that useful tidal tables have been constructed for centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The resulting amplitudes and phases can then be used to predict the expected tides. These are usually dominated by the constituents near 12&#160;hours (the <i>semi-diurnal</i> constituents), but there are major constituents near 24&#160;hours (<i>diurnal</i>) as well. Longer term constituents are 14&#160;day or <i>fortnightly</i>, monthly, and semiannual. Semi-diurnal tides dominated coastline, but some areas such as the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> are primarily diurnal. In the semi-diurnal areas, the primary constituents <i>M</i><sub>2</sub>&#160;(lunar) and <i>S</i><sub>2</sub>&#160;(solar) periods differ slightly, so that the relative phases, and thus the amplitude of the combined tide, change fortnightly (14&#160;day period).<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <i>M</i><sub>2</sub> plot above, each cotidal line differs by one hour from its neighbors, and the thicker lines show tides in phase with equilibrium at Greenwich. The lines rotate around the <a href="/wiki/Amphidromic_point" title="Amphidromic point">amphidromic points</a> counterclockwise in the northern hemisphere so that from <a href="/wiki/Baja_California_Peninsula" title="Baja California Peninsula">Baja California Peninsula</a> to <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> and from <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> the <i>M</i><sub>2</sub> tide propagates northward. In the southern hemisphere this direction is clockwise. On the other hand, <i>M</i><sub>2</sub> tide propagates counterclockwise around New Zealand, but this is because the islands act as a dam and permit the tides to have different heights on the islands\' opposite sides. (The tides do propagate northward on the east side and southward on the west coast, as predicted by theory.)\n</p><p>The exception is at <a href="/wiki/Cook_Strait" title="Cook Strait">Cook Strait</a> where the tidal currents periodically link high to low water. This is because cotidal lines 180\xc2\xb0 around the amphidromes are in opposite phase, for example high water across from low water at each end of Cook Strait. Each tidal constituent has a different pattern of amplitudes, phases, and amphidromic points, so the <i>M</i><sub>2</sub> patterns cannot be used for other tide components.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Example_calculation">Example calculation</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: The article on <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Thomas_Doodson" title="Arthur Thomas Doodson">A.T. Doodson</a> has a fully worked example calculation for <a href="/wiki/Bridgeport,_Connecticut" title="Bridgeport, Connecticut">Bridgeport, Connecticut</a>, U.S.A.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div Connecticut</a>, U.S.A. during a 50-hour period.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide.Bridgeport.30d.svg" class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide.Bridgeport.400d.svg" class="image"><img alt="Graph showing with a single line showing only a minimal annual tidal fluctuation" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Tide.Bridgeport.400d.svg/220px-Tide.Bridgeport.400d.svg.png" width="220" height="103" class="thumbimage"
