class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide.Bridgeport.400d.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tides at Bridgeport, Connecticut, U.S.A. during a 400-day period.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide.NZ.November.png" class="image"><img alt="Graph showing 6 lines with two lines for each of three cities. Nelson has two monthly spring tides, while Napier and Wellington each have one." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Tide.NZ.November.png/220px-Tide.NZ.November.png" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Tide.NZ.November.png/330px-Tide.NZ.November.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Tide.NZ.November.png/440px-Tide.NZ.November.png 2x" data-file-width="818" data-file-height="517" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide.NZ.November.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tidal patterns in Cook Strait. The north part (Nelson) has two spring tides per month, versus only one on the south side (Wellington and Napier).</div></div></div>\n<p>Because the Moon is moving in its orbit around the Earth and in the same sense as the Earth\'s rotation, a point on the Earth must rotate slightly further to catch up so that the time between semidiurnal tides is not twelve but 12.4206 hours\xe2\x80\x94a bit over twenty-five minutes extra. The two peaks are not equal. The two high tides a day alternate in maximum heights: lower high (just under three feet), higher high (just over three feet), and again lower high. Likewise for the low tides.\n</p><p>When the Earth, Moon, and Sun are in line (Sun\xe2\x80\x93Earth\xe2\x80\x93Moon, or Sun\xe2\x80\x93Moon\xe2\x80\x93Earth) the two main influences combine to produce spring tides; when the two forces are opposing each other as when the angle Moon\xe2\x80\x93Earth\xe2\x80\x93Sun is close to ninety degrees, neap tides result. As the Moon moves around its orbit it changes from north of the Equator to south of the Equator. The alternation in high tide heights becomes smaller, until they are the same (at the lunar equinox, the Moon is above the Equator), then redevelop but with the other polarity, waxing to a maximum difference and then waning again.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current">Current</span></h3>\n<p>The tides\' influence on current flow is much more difficult to analyse, and data is much more difficult to collect. A tidal height is a simple number which applies to a wide region simultaneously. A flow has both a magnitude and a direction, both of which can vary substantially with depth and over short distances due to local bathymetry. Also, although a water channel\'s center is the most useful measuring site, mariners object when current-measuring equipment obstructs waterways. A flow proceeding up a curved channel is the same flow, even though its direction varies continuously along the channel. Surprisingly, flood and ebb flows are often not in opposite directions. Flow direction is determined by the upstream channel\'s shape, not the downstream channel\'s shape. Likewise, <a href="/wiki/Eddy_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Eddy (fluid dynamics)">eddies</a> may form in only one flow direction.\n</p><p>Nevertheless, current analysis is similar to tidal analysis: in the simple case, at a given location the flood flow is in mostly one direction, and the ebb flow in another direction. Flood velocities are given positive sign, and ebb velocities negative sign. Analysis proceeds as though these are tide heights.\n</p><p>In more complex situations, the main ebb and flood flows do not dominate. Instead, the flow direction and magnitude trace an ellipse over a tidal cycle (on a polar plot) instead of along the ebb and flood lines. In this case, analysis might proceed along pairs of directions, with the primary and secondary directions at right angles. An alternative is to treat the tidal flows as complex numbers, as each value has both a magnitude and a direction.\n</p><p>Tide flow information is most commonly seen on <a href="/wiki/Nautical_chart" title="Nautical chart">nautical charts</a>, presented as a table of flow speeds and bearings at hourly intervals, with separate tables for spring and neap tides. The timing is relative to high water at some harbour where the tidal behaviour is similar in pattern, though it may be far away.\n</p><p>As with tide height predictions, tide flow predictions based only on astronomical factors do not incorporate weather conditions, which can <i>completely</i> change the outcome.\n</p><p>The tidal flow through <a href="/wiki/Cook_Strait" title="Cook Strait">Cook Strait</a> between the two main islands of New Zealand is particularly interesting, as the tides on each side of the strait are almost exactly out of phase, so that one side\'s high water is simultaneous with the other\'s low water. Strong currents result, with almost zero tidal height change in the strait\'s center. Yet, although the tidal surge normally flows in one direction for six hours and in the reverse direction for six hours, a particular surge might last eight or ten hours with the reverse surge enfeebled. In especially boisterous weather conditions, the reverse surge might be entirely overcome so that the flow continues in the same direction through three or more surge periods.\n</p><p>A further complication for Cook Strait\'s flow pattern is that the tide at the north side (e.g. at <a href="/wiki/Nelson,_New_Zealand" title="Nelson, New Zealand">Nelson</a>) follows the common bi-weekly spring\xe2\x80\x93neap tide cycle (as found along the west side of the country), but the south side\'s tidal pattern has only <i>one</i> cycle per month, as on the east side: <a href="/wiki/Wellington" title="Wellington">Wellington</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Napier,_New_Zealand" title="Napier, New Zealand">Napier</a>.\n</p><p>The graph of Cook Strait\'s tides shows separately the high water and low water height and time, through November 2007; these are <i>not</i> measured values but instead are calculated from tidal parameters derived from years-old measurements. Cook Strait\'s nautical chart offers tidal current information. For instance the January 1979 edition for 41\xc2\xb013\xc2\xb79\xe2\x80\x99S 174\xc2\xb029\xc2\xb76\xe2\x80\x99E (north west of <a href="/wiki/Cape_Terawhiti" title="Cape Terawhiti">Cape Terawhiti</a>) refers timings to <a href="/wiki/Westport,_New_Zealand" title="Westport, New Zealand">Westport</a> while the January 2004 issue refers to Wellington. Near Cape Terawhiti in the middle of Cook Strait the tidal height variation is almost nil while the tidal current reaches its maximum, especially near the notorious Karori Rip. Aside from weather effects, the actual currents through Cook Strait are influenced by the tidal height differences between the two ends of the strait and as can be seen, only one of the two spring tides at the north end (Nelson) has a counterpart spring tide at the south end (Wellington), so the resulting behaviour follows neither reference harbour.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_generation">Power generation</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">Tidal power</a></div>\n<p>Tidal energy can be extracted by two means: inserting a water <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">turbine</a> into a tidal current, or building ponds that release/admit water through a turbine. In the first case, the energy amount is entirely determined by the timing and tidal current magnitude. However, the best currents may be unavailable because the turbines would obstruct ships. In the second, the impoundment dams are expensive to construct, natural water cycles are completely disrupted, ship navigation is disrupted. However, with multiple ponds, power can be generated at chosen times. So far, there are few installed systems for tidal power generation (most famously, <a href="/wiki/Rance_tidal_power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Rance tidal power plant">La Rance</a> at <a href="/wiki/St_Malo" class="mw-redirect" title="St Malo">Saint Malo</a>, France) which face many difficulties. Aside from environmental issues, simply withstanding corrosion and biological fouling pose engineering challenges.\n</p><p>Tidal power proponents point out that, unlike wind power systems, generation levels can be reliably predicted, save for weather effects. While some generation is possible for most of the tidal cycle, in practice turbines lose efficiency at lower operating rates. Since the power available from a flow is proportional to the cube of the flow speed, the times during which high power generation is possible are brief.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Navigation">Navigation</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide_legal_use.gif" class="image"><img alt="Chart illustrating that tidal heights enter in calculations of legally significant data such as boundary lines between the high seas and territorial waters. Chart shows an exemplar coastline, identifying bottom features such as longshore bar and berms, tidal heights such as mean higher high water, and distances from shore such as the 12 mile limit." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Tide_legal_use.gif/220px-Tide_legal_use.gif" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Tide_legal_use.gif/330px-Tide_legal_use.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Tide_legal_use.gif/440px-Tide_legal_use.gif 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="477" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tide_legal_use.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>US civil and maritime uses of tidal data</div></div></div>\n<p>Tidal flows are important for navigation, and significant errors in position occur if they are not accommodated. Tidal heights are also important; for example many rivers and harbours have a shallow "bar" at the entrance which prevents boats with significant <a href="/wiki/Draft_(hull)" title="Draft (hull)">draft</a> from entering at low tide.\n</p><p>Until the advent of automated navigation, competence in calculating tidal effects was important to naval officers. The certificate of examination for lieutenants in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> once declared that the prospective officer was able to "shift his tides".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tidal flow timings and velocities appear in <i>tide charts</i> or a <a href="/wiki/Tidal_stream_atlas" class="mw-redirect" title="Tidal stream atlas">tidal stream atlas</a>. Tide charts come in sets. Each chart covers a single hour between one high water and another (they ignore the leftover 24 minutes) and show the average tidal flow for that hour. An arrow on the tidal chart indicates the direction and the average flow speed (usually in <a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">knots</a>) for spring and neap tides. If a tide chart is not available, most nautical charts have "<a href="/wiki/Tidal_diamond" title="Tidal diamond">tidal diamonds</a>" which relate specific points on the chart to a table giving tidal flow direction and speed.\n</p><p>The standard procedure to counteract tidal effects on navigation is to (1) calculate a "<a href="/wiki/Dead_reckoning" title="Dead reckoning">dead reckoning</a>" position (or DR) from travel distance and direction, (2) mark the chart (with a vertical cross like a plus sign) and (3) draw a line from the DR in the tide\'s direction. The distance the tide moves the boat along this line is computed by the tidal speed, and this gives an "estimated position" or EP (traditionally marked with a dot in a triangle).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tidal_Indicator_Delaware_River_ca1897.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Tidal_Indicator_Delaware_River_ca1897.jpg/220px-Tidal_Indicator_Delaware_River_ca1897.jpg" width="220" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Tidal_Indicator_Delaware_River_ca1897.jpg/330px-Tidal_Indicator_Delaware_River_ca1897.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Tidal_Indicator_Delaware_River_ca1897.jpg/440px-Tidal_Indicator_Delaware_River_ca1897.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="827" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tidal_Indicator_Delaware_River_ca1897.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tidal Indicator, Delaware River, Delaware c. 1897. At the time shown in the figure, the tide is &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap">1<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>4</sub></span> feet above mean low water and is still falling, as indicated by pointing of the arrow. Indicator is powered by system of pulleys, cables and a float. (Report Of The Superintendent Of The Coast &amp; Geodetic Survey Showing The Progress Of The Work During The Fiscal Year Ending With June 1897 (p. 483))</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Nautical_chart" title="Nautical chart">Nautical charts</a> display the water\'s "charted depth" at specific locations with "<a href="/wiki/Echo_sounding" title="Echo sounding">soundings</a>" and the use of bathymetric <a href="/wiki/Contour_line" title="Contour line">contour lines</a> to depict the submerged surface\'s shape. These depths are relative to a "<a href="/wiki/Chart_datum" title="Chart datum">chart datum</a>", which is typically the water level at the lowest possible astronomical tide (although other datums are commonly used, especially historically, and tides may be lower or higher for meteorological reasons) and are therefore the minimum possible water depth during the tidal cycle. "Drying heights" may also be shown on the chart, which are the heights of the exposed <a href="/wiki/Seabed" title="Seabed">seabed</a> at the lowest astronomical tide.\n</p><p>Tide tables list each day\'s high and low water heights and times. To calculate the actual water depth, add the charted depth to the published tide height. Depth for other times can be derived from tidal curves published for major ports. The <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_twelfths" title="Rule of twelfths">rule of twelfths</a> can suffice if an accurate curve is not available. This approximation presumes that the increase in depth in the six hours between low and high water is: first hour \xe2\x80\x94 1/12, second \xe2\x80\x94 2/12, third \xe2\x80\x94 3/12, fourth \xe2\x80\x94 3/12, fifth \xe2\x80\x94 2/12, sixth \xe2\x80\x94 1/12.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_aspects">Biological aspects</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intertidal_ecology">Intertidal ecology</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Intertide_zonation_at_Kalaloch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photo of partially submerged rock showing horizontal bands of different color and texture, where each band represents a different fraction of time spent submerged." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Intertide_zonation_at_Kalaloch.jpg/170px-Intertide_zonation_at_Kalaloch.jpg" width="170" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Intertide_zonation_at_Kalaloch.jpg/255px-Intertide_zonation_at_Kalaloch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Intertide_zonation_at_Kalaloch.jpg/340px-Intertide_zonation_at_Kalaloch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1131" data-file-height="1820" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Intertide_zonation_at_Kalaloch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A rock, seen at low water, exhibiting typical intertidal zonation.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Intertidal_ecology" title="Intertidal ecology">Intertidal ecology</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Intertidal_zone" title="Intertidal zone">Intertidal zone</a></div>\n<p>Intertidal ecology is the study of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a> between the low- and high-water lines along a shore. At low water, the intertidal zone is exposed (or <i>emersed</i>), whereas at high water, it is underwater (or <i>immersed</i>). Intertidal <a href="/wiki/Ecologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecologist">ecologists</a> therefore study the interactions between intertidal organisms and their environment, as well as among the <a href="/wiki/Biological_interaction" title="Biological interaction">different species</a>. The most important interactions may vary according to the type of intertidal community. The broadest classifications are based on substrates \xe2\x80\x94 <a href="/wiki/Rocky_shore" title="Rocky shore">rocky shore</a> or soft bottom.\n</p><p>Intertidal organisms experience a highly variable and often hostile environment, and have <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adapted</a> to cope with and even exploit these conditions. One easily visible feature is <a href="/wiki/Vertical_zonation" class="mw-redirect" title="Vertical zonation">vertical zonation</a>, in which the community divides into distinct horizontal bands of specific species at each elevation above low water. A species\' ability to cope with <a href="/wiki/Desiccation" title="Desiccation">desiccation</a> determines its upper limit, while <a href="/wiki/Competition_(biology)" title="Competition (biology)">competition</a> with other species sets its lower limit.\n</p><p>Humans <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_natural_resources" title="Exploitation of natural resources">use</a> <a href="/wiki/Intertidal_ecology" title="Intertidal ecology">intertidal regions</a> for food and recreation. <a href="/wiki/Overexploitation" title="Overexploitation">Overexploitation</a> can damage intertidals directly. Other anthropogenic actions such as introducing <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a> and <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">climate change</a> have large negative effects. <a href="/wiki/Marine_Protected_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="Marine Protected Area">Marine Protected Areas</a> are one option communities can apply to protect these areas and aid scientific <a href="/wiki/Research" title="Research">research</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_rhythms">Biological rhythms</span></h3>\n<p>The approximately fortnightly tidal cycle has large effects on intertidal<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> and marine organisms.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Hence their <a href="/wiki/Biological_rhythm" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological rhythm">biological rhythms</a> tend to occur in rough multiples of this period. Many other animals such as the <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>, display similar rhythms. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">gestation</a> and egg hatching. In humans, the <a href="/wiki/Menstrual_cycle" title="Menstrual cycle">menstrual cycle</a> lasts roughly a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_month" title="Lunar month">lunar month</a>, an even multiple of the tidal period. Such parallels at least hint at the <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">common descent</a> of all animals from a marine ancestor.<sup id="cite_ref-Descent_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Descent-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_tides">Other tides</span></h2>\n<p>When oscillating tidal currents in the stratified ocean flow over uneven bottom topography, they generate <a href="/wiki/Internal_wave" title="Internal wave">internal waves</a> with tidal frequencies. Such waves are called <i><a href="/wiki/Internal_tide" title="Internal tide">internal tides</a></i>.\n</p><p>Shallow areas in otherwise open water can experience rotary tidal currents, flowing in directions that continually change and thus the flow direction (not the flow) completes a full rotation in &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap">12<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> hours (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Nantucket_Shoals" title="Nantucket Shoals">Nantucket Shoals</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-rocur_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rocur-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to oceanic tides, large lakes can experience small tides and even planets can experience <i><a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_tide" title="Atmospheric tide">atmospheric tides</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Earth_tide" title="Earth tide">Earth tides</a></i>. These are <a href="/wiki/Continuum_mechanics" title="Continuum mechanics">continuum mechanical</a> phenomena. The first two take place in <a href="/wiki/Fluid_mechanics" title="Fluid mechanics">fluids</a>. The third affects the Earth\'s thin <a href="/wiki/Solid_mechanics" title="Solid mechanics">solid</a> crust surrounding its semi-liquid interior (with various modifications).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lake_tides">Lake tides</span></h3>\n<p>Large lakes such as <a href="/wiki/Lake_Superior" title="Lake Superior">Superior</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">Erie</a> can experience tides of 1 to 4&#160;cm (0.39 to 1.6&#160;in), but these can be masked by meteorologically induced phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Seiche" title="Seiche">seiche</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The tide in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">Lake Michigan</a> is described as 0.5 to 1.5 inches (13 to 38&#160;mm)<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> or &#x200b;<span class="frac nowrap">1<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>3</sup>&#8260;<sub>4</sub></span>&#160;inches.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\nThis is so small that other larger effects completely mask any tide, and as such these lakes are considered non-tidal.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_tides">Atmospheric tides</span></h3>\n<p>Atmospheric tides are negligible at ground level and aviation altitudes, masked by <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a>\'s much more important effects. Atmospheric tides are both gravitational and thermal in origin and are the dominant dynamics from about 80 to 120 kilometres (50 to 75&#160;mi), above which the molecular density becomes too low to support fluid behavior.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_tides">Earth tides</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earth_tide" title="Earth tide">Earth tide</a></div>\n<p>Earth tides or terrestrial tides affect the entire Earth\'s mass, which acts similarly to a liquid <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscope</a> with a very thin crust. The Earth\'s crust shifts (in/out, east/west, north/south) in response to lunar and solar gravitation, ocean tides, and atmospheric loading. While negligible for most human activities, terrestrial tides\' semi-diurnal amplitude can reach about 55 centimetres (22&#160;in) at the Equator\xe2\x80\x9415 centimetres (5.9&#160;in) due to the Sun\xe2\x80\x94which is important in <a href="/wiki/GPS" class="mw-redirect" title="GPS">GPS</a> calibration and <a href="/wiki/VLBI" class="mw-redirect" title="VLBI">VLBI</a> measurements. Precise astronomical angular measurements require knowledge of the Earth\'s rotation rate and <a href="/wiki/Polar_motion" title="Polar motion">polar motion</a>, both of which are influenced by Earth tides. The semi-diurnal <i>M</i><sub>2</sub> Earth tides are nearly in phase with the Moon with a lag of about two hours.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galactic_tides">Galactic tides</span></h3>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/Galactic_tide" title="Galactic tide">Galactic tides</a></i> are the tidal forces exerted by galaxies on stars within them and <a href="/wiki/Satellite_galaxy" title="Satellite galaxy">satellite galaxies</a> orbiting them. The galactic tide\'s effects on the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a> are believed to cause 90 percent of long-period comets.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Misnomers">Misnomers</span></h2>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">Tsunamis</a>, the large waves that occur after earthquakes, are sometimes called <i>tidal waves</i>, but this name is given by their <i>resemblance</i> to the tide, rather than any actual link to the tide. Other phenomena unrelated to tides but using the word <i>tide</i> are <a href="/wiki/Rip_current" title="Rip current">rip tide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Storm_tide" class="mw-redirect" title="Storm tide">storm tide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_tide" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurricane tide">hurricane tide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oil_spill" title="Oil spill">black</a> or <a href="/wiki/Red_tide" title="Red tide">red tides</a>.  Many of these usages are historic and refer to the earlier meaning of tide as "a portion of time, a season".<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright"
